<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="b8a8bf4f093943f08d5434e96d2a0f88"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="b8a8bf4f093943f08d5434e96d2a0f88">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '7e7ea987-1a91-412c-9bf4-e24eff5777bc'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                     <p>These authors contributed equally to this work.</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Long/Hongli">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Wang/Jing" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a2" data-db-target-for="a2" aria-controls="a2" aria-haspopup="true" id="a2_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Prof. Jing Wang</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a2" aria-labelledby="a2_Ctrl" role="region" id="a2">
                     <p class="author-name">Prof. Jing Wang</p>
                     <p>Hebei Key Laboratory of Heavy Metal Deep-Remediation in Water and Resource Reuse, Yanshan University, Qinhuangdao, 066004 China</p>
                     <p>These authors contributed equally to this work.</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Wang/Jing">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Zhao/Shengyu" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a3" data-db-target-for="a3" aria-controls="a3" aria-haspopup="true" id="a3_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Shengyu Zhao</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a3" aria-labelledby="a3_Ctrl" role="region" id="a3">
                     <p class="author-name">Shengyu Zhao</p>
                     <p>College of Science &amp; Institute for Sustainable Energy, Shanghai University, Shanghai, 200444 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Zhao/Shengyu">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Zou/Bobo" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a4" data-db-target-for="a4" aria-controls="a4" aria-haspopup="true" id="a4_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Bobo Zou</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a4" aria-labelledby="a4_Ctrl" role="region" id="a4">

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d58803915" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>In this work, we report a novel particle-in-bulk inlaid architecture with bismuth nanospheres enclosed in an N-doped bulk carbon (BiNC). Such a structure can enable the reversible Na<sub>3</sub>Bi↔layered Bi phase transition triggered by the compressive stress-induced “domino” effect, thus suppress the irreversible structural evolutions to achieve an extraordinary cyclic stability.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e3a1464b-9a1b-43bb-8fda-4b234cf1e170/anie202406513-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e3a1464b-9a1b-43bb-8fda-4b234cf1e170/anie202406513-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2a2234d8-6cfb-42e9-9b8a-85c7af9e2afb/anie202406513-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e3a1464b-9a1b-43bb-8fda-4b234cf1e170/anie202406513-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d58803952" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Alloying-type anodes show capacity and density advantages for sodium/potassium-ion batteries (SIBs/PIBs), but they encounter serious structural degradation upon cycling, which cannot be resolved through conventional nanostructuring techniques. Herein, we present an in-depth study to reveal the intrinsic reason for the pulverization of bismuth (Bi) materials upon (de)alloying, and report a novel particle-in-bulk architecture with Bi nanospheres inlaid in the bulk carbon (BiNC) to achieve durable Na/K storage. We simulate the volume-expansion-resistant mechanism of Bi during the (de)alloying reaction, and unveil that the irreversible phase transition upon (de)alloying underlies the fundamental origin for the structural degradation of Bi anode, while a proper compressive stress (~10 %) raised by the bulk carbon can trigger a “domino-like” Bi crystal recovering. Consequently, the as obtained BiNC exhibits a record high volumetric capacity (823.1 mAh cm<sup>−3</sup> for SIBs, 848.1 mAh cm<sup>−3</sup> for PIBs) and initial coulombic efficiency (95.3 % for SIBs, 96.4 % for PIBs), and unprecedented cycling stability (15000 cycles for SIBs with only 0.0015 % degradation per cycle), outperforming the state-of-the-art literature. This work provides new insights on the undesirable structural evolution, and proposes basic guidelines for design of the anti-degradation structure for alloy-type electrode materials.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202406513-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202406513-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Rechargeable sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (PIBs) have been considered as complementary and viable alternatives to lithium-ion batteries for electric vehicles and grid-scale energy storage applications, owing to the abundant Na/K resources and commendable energy densities. Nevertheless, the practical applications of SIBs/PIBs demand suitable anode materials that can adapt to the relatively larger ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) and K<sup>+</sup> (1.38 Å) as compared to Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0001_R_d58803839e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Currently, carbon-based materials including hard carbons (HCs) or graphite are the most widely explored anode materials, which however usually demonstrate very small tap densities (e.g.≤0.9 g.cm<sup>−3</sup> for HCs) and hence very low volumetric capacities, low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE), which represents a significant bottleneck for the large-scale application of SIBs/PIBs. Alloying-type anodes (forming A<sub>x</sub>M<sub>y</sub>, A=Na, K; M=Sn, P, Sb, Bi, etc) with high theoretical capacities and volumetric density advantages over carbon-based material, are considered as attractive candidates for the next-generation sodium-ion (SIBs) and potassium-ion (PIBs) batteries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0007_R_d58803839e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> Among them, the low-cost and nontoxic bismuth with broad interlayer spacing for Na<sup>+</sup> accommodation and high volumetric capacity (3800 mAh L<sup>−1</sup>) have been extensively studied. However, its two-phase (de)alloying process would cause massive mechanical stress, which leads to the large volume expansion (242 % for SIBs, 411 % for PIBs), irreversible structural evolution and finally unavoidable electrode pulverization. Therefore, to develop a stress-resilient structure of the alloy-type materials, which could decouple the strong chemo mechanical effects from battery cycling, becomes the prerequisite for their practical applications.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0011_R_d58803839e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Efforts have been devoted to address the huge volume changes during charge/discharge, including reducing the Bi particle size to nanoscale, or compositing Bi with carbon materials to form a core–shell or yolk-shell structure, etc.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0017_R_d58803839e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> For instance, a Bi@N-doped carbon with a multi-core-shell structure has been reported, which showed superior cyclability with an unprecedented high capacity of 235 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> in SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0019_R_d58803839e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4b - Adv. Funct. Mater.">4b</a></sup></span> Further studies enclosed Bi nanoparticles with a carbon-nanosheet framework, which delivered an outstanding rate capability (232 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> @ 60 A g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0020_R_d58803839e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span> Bulk Bi has also been investigated as the SIB electrode, which performed both high rate and remarkable cycling abilities (4000 cycles).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0021_R_d58803839e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span> The application of Bi for potassium storage was also reported to demonstrate an outstanding electrochemical performance (345 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> @ 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0022_R_d58803839e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span> More recently, a quasi-1D structured NiBi<sub>3</sub> was developed, which achieves stable cycling performance for potassium ion storage (287.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0023_R_d58803839e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, both bulk, nanostructured, and dimensionally modified Bi would undergo an irreversible structural evolution and even form porous-like structure upon charge/discharge, which brings forth severe volume expansion and subsequent pulverization, and meanwhile causes repeated dissolving and formation of unstable SEI (solid electrolyte interface) on the particle surface.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0024_R_d58803839e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In this work, we report a unique particle-in-bulk structure with Bi nanospheres inlaid in dense bulk N doped C (BiNC), which demonstrates an extremely fast-charging capability, ultra-high initial coulombic efficiency, and ultra stable long cycle stability for SIBs/PIBs. We unveil that the unique particle-in-bulk inlaid architecture guarantees that the stress and strain caused by the volume change could be transferred from Bi nanospheres to the surrounded carbon. More intriguingly, we simulate the volume-expansion-resistant mechanism of bismuth during the dealloying reaction with varying degrees of stiffness (corresponding to various out-of-plane stresses) and confirm that, certain compressive stress raised by the bulk carbon would enable a “domino-like” Bi crystal recovering, thus keeping the notable stability of the structure. This work provides new understanding on the undesirable pulverization, and sheds lights on practical applications of alloying-type anode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202406513-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202406513-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202406513-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202406513-sec-0003-title"> Preparation and Characterization of Bulk Structured BiNC</h3>
            
            <p>Using bismuth ammonium salt complex (Bi (NH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>7</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, BAC) as the precursor, the Bi nanospheres embedded in N doped C inlaid-structure composites (BiNC) were obtained through a simple two-step process as illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0001">1a</a>. The morphology and structure of BiNC composite were investigated using scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). The BAC precursor showed a compact bulk structure with a smooth surface, sizing around 30–90 μm (Figure S1, Supporting Information). The as-obtained BiNC composite maintains the bulk morphology, with the particle size reduced to 2–12 μm (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0001">1b</a> and <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0001">c</a>, and Figure S2, Supporting Information), and ultrafine Bi nanospheres (5–25 nm) are uniformly embedded within the bulk carbon (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0001">1d</a> and <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0001">e</a>). The crystal structure of the sample was characterized by the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM), whereby the lattice fringes can be indexed to the (012) crystal planes of hexagonal metallic Bi (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0001">1f</a>), and the corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED) diffraction rings are shown in Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0001">1g</a>. The crystallographic structures of BiNC composites were further confirmed by X-ray diffraction patterns (XRD), which demonstrate characteristic sharp peaks of the hexagonal bismuth phase with the <i>R-3 m</i> space group (JCPDS: 85–1329) (Figure S3, Supporting Information). The energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping profile unambiguously identifies the homogeneous distribution of Bi, C, and N elements in the BiNC composite (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0001">1h</a>–<a href="#anie202406513-fig-0001">1k</a>). The volumes of several anode materials with the same mass were compared (Figure S4, Supporting Information). In comparison to hard carbon (~0.85 g cm<sup>−3</sup>) and other anode materials, BiNC has an elevated tap density of 1.97 g cm<sup>−3</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0001">1l</a> and Table S1, Supporting Information), which makes it a more competitive candidate for the volume sensitive applications. The elemental information of BiNC were also verified by the X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectra (Figure S5, Supporting Information). The Raman spectrum of BiNC was conducted to examine the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio (1.02), indicating the relatively low defect content in BiNC (Figure S6, Supporting Information). In addition, the percentage of Bi in BiNC was determined as 79.7 wt % from the thermal gravimetric analysis result (TGA) (Figure S7, Supporting Information). The specific surface area of BiNC was tested as 14.65 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, which is smaller than those in literature (Figure S8, Supporting Information).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0028_R_d58803839e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span> For comparison, pure Bi samples were synthesized according to the method previously reported as control sample, the corresponding structure was also characterized and shown in Figure S9, Supporting Information.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0022_R_d58803839e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span>
               </p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202406513-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f4e818c4-be75-406a-b989-e313a4dc9c98/anie202406513-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f4e818c4-be75-406a-b989-e313a4dc9c98/anie202406513-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8412ece2-9f76-4b9b-86f2-4e5b2456e2f7/anie202406513-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f4e818c4-be75-406a-b989-e313a4dc9c98/anie202406513-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202406513-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202406513-sec-0004-title"> Electrochemical Performance of BiNC</h3>
            
            <p>The Na-storage properties of the BiNC composites were investigated in coin-type half-cells. The representative cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of BiNC (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0002">2a</a>) show reversible redox peaks at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, which are assigned to the Na−Bi (de)alloying processes. Unlike other bismuth-based electrodes previously reported, only one cathodic peak at 0.46 V appeared during the initial discharge scan, On the contrary, the CV curves of the pure Bi (Figure S10a, Supporting Information) show four peaks at 0.68 V/0.48 V and 0.76 V/0.60 V in the first scan. The galvanostatic charge/discharge curve (GCD) profiles of BiNC and Bi were measured at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S10, Supporting Information). Similarly, the BiNC demonstrated only one obvious voltage plateau at 0.47 V, which is different from that of the control sample (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0002">2b</a> <i>and</i> Figure S10b, Supporting Information). Two flat plateaus at 0.62 V and 0.77 V were observed in the first charge curve, which indicates two processes characteristic of the dealloying process of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0032_R_d58803839e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span> Specifically, the BiNC electrode provides a charge/discharge capacity of 398/417.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponds to a high volumetric capacity of 823.1 mAh cm<sup>−3</sup>, which is much higher than that of hard carbons (256.7 mAh cm<sup>−3</sup>) and other Bi nanocomposites (Table S1, Supporting Information). The ICE reached a pleasantly surprising value of 95.3 %, which stands out compared with those of previously reported anode materials (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0002">2c</a>) and that of pure Bi sample (77.8 %, Figure S10, Supporting Information). This extraordinary ICE value for BiNC should be attributed to its ultrasmall specific surface area. The rate performance of the sample was further investigated at various current densities from 1 to 100 A g<sup>−1</sup> (~260 C, corresponding to an ultrafast charge time of ~14 s), (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0002">2d</a>), whereby high reversible capacities of 410.8 to 290.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> were obtained with a high-capacity retention of 70.3 %. The GCD curves at different current densities indicate the polarization voltage increases with increasing current density, but still shows two distinct charging and discharging plateaus (Figure S11b, Supporting Information). The rate capability of BiNC is significantly superior to that of Bi-based anodes previously reported (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0002">2e</a>). The as prepared BiNC sample demonstrates a record-high long cycle life with high-capacity retention of 320.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 15,000 cycles at a high current density of 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> with only 0.0015 % degradation per cycle (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0002">2f</a>). Such promising electrochemical performance surpasses the pure Bi control sample and the state-of-the-art Bi-based composites and metal phosphides anodes reported in literatures (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">3g</a> and Table S2, Supporting Information).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0019_R_d58803839e671" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4b - Adv. Funct. Mater.">4b</a>, <a href="#anie202406513-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0035_R_d58803839e671" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> Promisingly, the low-temperature performance of the BiNC was tested at an extremely low temperature of −40 °C, revealing an ultra-high capacity of 382.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and slight polarization, which is superior to the state-of-the art literatures (Figure S12, Supporting Information).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0059_R_d58803839e678" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a></sup></span> The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) test (Figure S13, Supporting Information) to verify the fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in BiNC. A full cell with the BiNC anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C(NVP@C) cathode was constructed (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0002">2h</a>), and denoted as BiNC//NVP@C.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202406513-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/de0f5c28-7cae-412c-9de6-4bd93bcc1836/anie202406513-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/de0f5c28-7cae-412c-9de6-4bd93bcc1836/anie202406513-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a145ecf4-004c-45e5-b30e-d5f2e002b870/anie202406513-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/de0f5c28-7cae-412c-9de6-4bd93bcc1836/anie202406513-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202406513-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/693fc244-edb9-4fd7-8883-40f1dce4cee5/anie202406513-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/693fc244-edb9-4fd7-8883-40f1dce4cee5/anie202406513-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fb7c304f-737f-4c4c-82ae-0572d443dd4a/anie202406513-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/693fc244-edb9-4fd7-8883-40f1dce4cee5/anie202406513-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202406513-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202406513" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Comprehensive Understanding on the Sodium Storage Mechanism through EIS and DRT Analysis. (c) Voltage profile. In situ EIS of (d) BiNC and (e) Bi electrodes during the 1st in diglyme electrolyte, respectively.                   </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Depending on the mass of anode, the full cell delivered a specific discharge capacity up to 314.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with a remarkable cycling ability of 82.0% capacity retention after 1000 cycles at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0002">2i</a>). The BiNC//NVP@C full cell showed two voltages at 2.4 V and 2.7 V, respectively, corresponding to the voltage difference between the NVP@C cathode and BiNC composite anode (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0002">2i</a>). In further, the BiNC//NVP@C full cell has shown excellent rate performance, offering reversible discharge capacities of 81.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 20 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S14, Supporting Information). Additionally, a remarkable energy density of 226.5 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and a maximum power density of 9.44 kW kg<sup>−1</sup> were achieved (based on the total mass of the anode and cathode active materials) (Figure S15, Supporting Information). In addition, the potassium storage performances of the BiNC electrode were further investigated. The BiNC anode displays a record high ICE (96.4 %), an ultra-high reversible capacity of 430.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (volumetric capacity of 848.1 mAh cm<sup>−3</sup>) at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, remarkable cycling performance (342.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1600 cycles with 0.0091 % capacity degradation per cycle) and ultrahigh rate capability (344.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>) in potassium-ion battery (see detailed description in Figure S16 and Table S3, Supporting Information).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0019_R_d58803839e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4b - Adv. Funct. Mater.">4b</a>, <a href="#anie202406513-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0022_R_d58803839e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#anie202406513-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0027_R_d58803839e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9c - ACS Nano">9c</a>, <a href="#anie202406513-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0042_R_d58803839e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12g - J. Mater. Chem. A">12g</a>, <a href="#anie202406513-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0064_R_d58803839e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202406513-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202406513-sec-0005-title"> Kinetic Characteristics During Sodiation/Desodiation in Na-Bi Batteries</h3>
            
            <p>Electrochemical impedance spectrum (EIS) measurement was conducted to investigate the interfacial charge transfer kinetics in BiNC and Bi electrodes as well as the SEI layer evolution mechanism upon charge/discharge.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0072_R_d58803839e800" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Rev.">15</a></sup></span> The impedance evolutions are divided into several stages, as displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">3a</a>. The R<sub>0</sub> corresponds to the internal resistance including the resistances of the electrolyte solution and electrodes, while the semi-circles at high and medium frequency are attributed to resistance associated with the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> in the interfacial layer (R<sub>SEI</sub>) and the charge transfer (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) related to the electrochemical reaction. The R<sub>0</sub>, R<sub>SEI</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values were quantified with an equivalent circuit (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">3a</a>). To differentiate distinct electrochemical processes while avoiding manual simulation, DRT technology is utilized, which is a model-free investigation into impedance evolutions (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">3b</a>). The schematic mechanism of DRT is displayed in Figure S17, Supporting Information.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0073" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0073_R_d58803839e830" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16">16</a></sup></span> The τ at 10<sup>−5</sup> s represents the system resistance (R<sub>0</sub>). The influence of the counter electrode can be excluded due to the weak response by DRT. Based on timescale analyses, three major peaks are identified at 10<sup>−5</sup> to 10<sup>−4</sup>, 10<sup>−3</sup> to 10<sup>−1</sup>, and 1 to 10 s exhibit obvious electrochemical processes for BiNC//Na, which were attributed to the ionic transport at the interfacial SEI (R<sub>SEI</sub>), the charge transfer processes (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and diffusion impedance (W<sub>diff</sub>) (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">3b</a>), respectively.</p>
            
            <p>The in situ EIS test was conducted for the BiNC electrode and the control sample at a current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> in the first two cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">3c</a>–<a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">e</a>). The variation tendencies of R<sub>SEI</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> could be simulated and are plotted in Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">3f</a> and <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">g</a>, respectively. It should be mentioned that with the BiNC, no noticeable increase in the impedance was observed during the first cycle despite the expected SEI layer formation (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">3f</a>). In contrast, the R<sub>SEI</sub> of pure Bi material first increases and then decreases in the first cycle, corresponding to the SEI to repeated the growth and dissolution of SEI during the electrochemical process. It is worth noting that R<sub>SEI</sub> was slightly (0.49 Ω for BiNC <i>v.s</i>. 3.95 Ω for pure Bi) increased for BiNC after the first charge/discharge cycle, indicating the very few SEI was formed, which corresponds well with the high ICE. The fluctuation trend of R<sub>SEI</sub> was reduced in the second cycle for the BiNC, implying no new SEI were formed in the second cycle (Figure S18, Supporting Information). The DRT graphics also demonstrates the good SEI stability in BiNC materials (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">3h</a>–<a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">3k</a>). Besides, BiNC has a much smaller charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) than Bi during the whole discharging/charging process, indicating a faster electrochemical reaction kinetic of BiNC (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">3g</a> and Figure S18, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">3l</a>–<a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">3n</a>, the resistance of the anode in BiNC was lower than that in Bi at the same cycling number, indicating the good interfacial stability. After 1000 cycles, the apparently increased impedance of the anode was observed in Bi, owing to the unstable electrode/electrolyte interface and pulverized electrodes. Surprisingly, it could be found that the R<sub>SEI</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> in BiNC electrode are more stable (&lt;8 Ω) (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0003">3n</a>), verifying that the BiNC structure could enable a sustainably stable SEI on BiNC electrode surface and enhance the interfacial charge transfer kinetics.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202406513-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202406513-sec-0006-title"> Morphological Evolution of the BiNC and Interface Chemistry</h3>
            
            <p>To have an in-depth understanding of the unprecedented cycling stability of BiNC upon, the structural evolution of the electrode material before and after cycling was investigated through ex situ SEM and TEM observations. The morphological evolution of the BiNC electrodes is schematically shown in Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0004">4a</a>. The BiNC electrode progressively transformed into a porous network structure as the cycle increased, and ultimately the carbon framework fell apart into nanosheet morphology (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0004">4b</a>–<a href="#anie202406513-fig-0004">4e</a>). Ex situ TEM images demonstrate that, the Bi nanospheres remain their original shapes, despite the surrounded carbon framework gradually loosen and became porous after 20 and 500 cycles. (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0004">4f</a>–<a href="#anie202406513-fig-0004">4i</a>). This indicates that part of the stresses induced by the (de)alloying of Bi during cycling were efficiently transferred to the surrounding carbon, preventing the pulverization of the Bi nanospheres.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0076" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0076_R_d58803839e944" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">17</a></sup></span> In contrast, increasing cycling causes structural stress changes in pure Bi, resulting in a constant refinement of the material particle size and a continual growth of the SEI film (Figure S19, Supporting Information).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0077" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0077_R_d58803839e947" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Commun.">18</a></sup></span>
               </p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202406513-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/82204e7e-df79-40f0-b867-023f2c61a23c/anie202406513-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/82204e7e-df79-40f0-b867-023f2c61a23c/anie202406513-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bfa9e901-8900-4ea2-82fc-2760d03ed520/anie202406513-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/82204e7e-df79-40f0-b867-023f2c61a23c/anie202406513-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202406513-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202406513" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Structural evolution of the BiNC and chemical composition of SEI formed was analyzed after cycles. (a) Schematic illustration of morphological evolution of the BiNC electrode during cycling and the chemical compositions and structures to the volume expansion of SEIs derived from BiNC and Bi. (b to e) SEM images of the pristine electrodes and those after 20, 200, and 500 cycles, respectively. (f to i) HRTEM images of the pristine electrodes and those after 20, 200, and 500 cycles for SIBs, respectively. Composition/nanostructure of SEIs probed by cryo-TEM and XPS. (j to m) cryo-TEM images of the BiNC electrodes and pure Bi electrodes after 200 cycles. (n) In-depth XPS spectra of C 1s, O 1s and F 1s of SEIs in BiNC (top) and pure Bi (down).</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The differences of electrochemical features are derived from SEI form, which is unraveled by using cryo-TEM to analyze the susceptible nanostructure of the SEIs without beam damage (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0004">4j</a>–4 m). Thickness of the SEI layer formed on the BiNC surface (1.2 nm) is much thinner than that of pure Bi (4.1 nm) after 200 cycles, which benefits the faster interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> transportation kinetics, and contributes to higher CE and rate capability. To further Figure out the chemical species in SEIs, ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was applied (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0004">4n</a>). Considerable parts of O−C−O, C−C−O and C−C−C are corresponded to polymer-like species in the diglyme solvent. The C 1s and O 1s spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0004">4n</a>, top) reveal that the main components of SEI layer are polyether (284.9 eV) and sodium alkoxides (R−CH<sub>2</sub>−O−Na) (532.2 eV). Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and NaF are the main and minor inorganic components in both SEIs, respectively. Notably, the SEI layers on the BiNC surfaces included more R−CH<sub>2</sub>−O−Na (20.6 %) and NaF (13.7 %) concentration than Bi (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0004">4n</a>, bottom). The SEI layers on the pure Bi surfaces had A greater Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (22.1 %) concentration than BiNC (9.8 %). In contrast, extensive crystal areas (Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>) are commonly generated for SEI on pure Bi anodes by breakdown of organic ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na, which is compatible with the XPS data.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0078" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0078_R_d58803839e1009" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Joule">19</a></sup></span> On the BiNC surfaces, an ultrathin SEI develops, with amorphous inorganic particles scattered in a polymer-like matrix. In addition, TEM EDS line scanning for the surface of BiNC after cycling showed featured peak intensities for F, O, and Na elements are uniformly distributed in surface layers (Figure S20, Supporting Information). This ultrathin SEI has exceptional stability against the significant volume change of alloy electrodes, thus providing good rate performance and cycle stability.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202406513-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202406513-sec-0007-title"> Comprehensive Understanding on the Sodium Storage Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>In previous literature, bismuth-based anodes have the identical reaction mechanism, i.e. the presence of two flat voltage plateaus during the first discharge, related to the two-step sodiation of Bi (Step I: Bi→NaBi and Step II: NaBi→Na<sub>3</sub>Bi). Accordingly, two flat voltage plateaus appear in the first charge process, corresponding to the inverse process of the discharge.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0029_R_d58803839e1024" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10a - Adv. Mater.">10a</a>, <a href="#anie202406513-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0031_R_d58803839e1024" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10c - Small">10c</a>, <a href="#anie202406513-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0038_R_d58803839e1024" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12c - Nano Lett.">12c</a>, <a href="#anie202406513-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0043_R_d58803839e1024" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12h - Adv. Mater.">12h</a>, <a href="#anie202406513-bib-0079" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0079_R_d58803839e1024" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Power Sources">20</a></sup></span> Here, we re-investigated the Na storage mechanism for the BiNC electrode through the in situ XRD and in situ TEM techniques (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0005">5</a> and Figure S21, Supporting Information). For convenience, we define two peculiar regions as stage I (~0.7 V) and stage II (~0.47 V) respectively. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0005">5a</a>, when the anode is discharged to 0.71 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), the diffraction peaks of Bi become weakened and simultaneously a small amount of NaBi can be detected (at 31.9°). As the anode is discharged to 0.48 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), the Na<sub>3</sub>Bi phase begins to appear at the expense of both Bi and NaBi (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0005">5b</a>). It should be noted that NaBi phase cannot solely exist during the whole process. Therefore, the stage II for BiNC corresponds to both Bi→Na<sub>3</sub>Bi and NaBi→Na<sub>3</sub>Bi reactions, which distinguishes our mechanism from the previous literature, and the control sample (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0005">5c</a> and <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0005">5d</a>). In the following charging, when the anode is charged back to 0.58 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), the peaks of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi gradually decline owing to the dealloying of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi towards the formation of Bi. Notably, even the Bi phase starts to appear before the Na<sub>3</sub>Bi and NaBi peaks have completely disappeared, suggesting the co-existence of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi, NaBi and Bi. This indicates that partial Na<sub>3</sub>Bi was directly converted back to Bi, which is different from that of the pure Bi sample (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0005">5c</a> and <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0005">5d</a>). The co-existence of the above three phases were also evidenced by the in situ TEM (Figure S22, Supporting Information). These in situ characterization results evidence the partial direct transformation between Bi and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0005">5e</a>), which might be responsible for the remarkably high ICE and unprecedented long cycle performance of the BiNC sample. Furthermore, the XRD patterns of the BiNC composite electrode after 1000 cycles, the peaks of rhombohedral phase Bi are obviously presented, confirming the existence of crystalline Bi and the excellent reversibility (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0005">5f</a> and 5 g). Meanwhile, the BiNC electrode remains its original morphology, while the Bi electrode shows sever cracks after 1000 cycles (Figure S23, Supporting Information).
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202406513-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d34c5a7d-7d55-419c-aabb-f3ec2ba14e61/anie202406513-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d34c5a7d-7d55-419c-aabb-f3ec2ba14e61/anie202406513-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9558f4a7-724f-407f-be56-7d2113c6c2aa/anie202406513-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d34c5a7d-7d55-419c-aabb-f3ec2ba14e61/anie202406513-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202406513-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202406513" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Further understanding of sodium storage mechanisms through in situ and ex situ observations. (a) The GCD curves of BiNC electrode during the in situ XRD test and corresponding 2D color-filled contour plot. (b) the amplified XRD pattern of the main peaks in the BiNC electrode during charge and discharge. (c) The GCD curves of pure Bi electrode during the in situ XRD test and corresponding 2D color-filled contour plot. (d) the amplified XRD pattern of the main peaks in the BiNC electrode during charge and discharge. (e) Schematic illustration of the alloying/dealloying mechanism of Bi. XRD patterns of cycled. (f) BiNC electrodes and (g) Bi electrodes (The inset images are corresponding digital photographs).</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202406513-sec-0008">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202406513-sec-0008-title"> Insights Into the Sodium Storage Behavior of BiNC at the Atomic Level</h3>
            
            <p>To gain atomic-level insights into the structural evolutions and electrochemical properties of electrode materials, we conducted systematic first principles calculations on Bi and its ultimate alloying compound Na<sub>3</sub>Bi. For starters, bulk Bi is composed of puckered sheets with an average layer-to-layer distance of 3.95 Å, and the calculated lattice parameters are <i>a</i>=<i>b</i>=4.546 Å and <i>c</i>=11.802 Å, in well accordance with experiment value. In the final alloying compound Na<sub>3</sub>Bi, the layered structure of Bi disappeared. It is found that the distance between in-plane adjacent Bi atoms is enlarged anisotropic ally, i.e., the in-plane distance expands 120.4 % while the out-of-plane distance is elongated to 244.1 % compared with the initial value (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0006">6a</a>). This means that most of the expansion during battery operation originates from the <i>c</i>-axis. To simulate the dealloying process, all Na atoms are removed from Na<sub>3</sub>Bi and the left Bi skeleton are optimized in a 5*5*6 supercell. It is revealed that the layered architecture cannot be reproduced when the structure is fully relaxed, whereby the Bi atoms remains the arrangement as in Na<sub>3</sub>Bi and form tetragonal P4/mmm symmetry (structure I, <i>a</i>=<i>b</i>=17.961 Å and <i>c</i>=35.024 Å) (Figure S24 and Table S4, Supporting Information). It is also noticed that the volume of structure I is larger than that of bulk Bi with same Bi atoms, which simulates us to do a further calculation loading finite strain. The strain is chosen to be along c direction considering the above-mentioned large anisotropic extension, and the magnitude is defined as (c-c<sub>0</sub>)/c<sub>0</sub>% (a negative value manifest compression). However, the architecture also converges in P4/mmm symmetry when strain is involved and it is independent on the magnitude of the strain (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0006">6b</a>).
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202406513-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7de55e40-7c62-40bf-a73d-1a39a6adc037/anie202406513-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7de55e40-7c62-40bf-a73d-1a39a6adc037/anie202406513-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6549b18b-e513-484b-91bb-9561d515e844/anie202406513-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7de55e40-7c62-40bf-a73d-1a39a6adc037/anie202406513-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202406513-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202406513" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Insights into the sodium storage behavior characterizations of BiNC at the atomic level. (a) Schematics of structural evolution of Bi to Na<sub>3</sub>Bi during electrochemical sodiation, and Purple: Bi; Yellow: Na. (b) With the increase of out-of-plane strain, structure evolution from the structure after all Na atoms are extracted. (c) Schematic illustrations of the degradation mechanisms and morphology evolution of the charge/discharge process in different Bi-based anodes including bare Bi and BiNC.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>It is aware that different Bi atoms will endure different strain in view of different distance from carbon layers. In other words, strain gradient exists in real condition which is different from above simulation whereby the strain value that all Bi atoms born is identical. Therefore, it is reasonable to suppose that the Bi atoms close to the carbon layer bears more strain and the displacement is larger. As a consequence, these Bi atoms will form layer preferentially in the vicinity of carbon layer. An interesting finding is that, when the structure optimization is conducted with an already-formed Bi layer, the bulk Bi layer structure can be achieved when out-of-plane strain reaches −10 %. The equilibrium volume for this obtained layered structure (Structure II) is <i>a</i>=<i>b</i>=21.0195 Å <i>c</i>=28.0757 Å, close to those of bulk Bi. Apparently, these lattice parameters are different from Structure I and it is lower in energy by 76.75 meV/Bi. With the strain being further enlarged, the structure II is stable within the stain of −11 %~−20 %. To load strain larger than −20 %, the lattice c is compressed to be smaller than that in the equilibrium structure, yet the layer structure remains after optimized. However, with the strain over −27 %, the distance between layers with be narrowed severely and the inter-layer Bi will interact with each other, destroying the layer structure. Upon the above simulation, we can understand the recovering of the bulk Bi structure in the dealloying process as a <i>strain assistant Bi-layer “domino”</i> (Figure <a href="#anie202406513-fig-0006">6c</a>).</p>
            
            <p>Henceforth, for the BiNC sample, the rigid carbon framework can provide certain out-of-plane strain during charging/discharging, which enables the formation of the first (few) Bi layers next to the surrounded carbon layer, and this will trigger a “<i>domino</i>” effect which induces the adjacent Bi atom to form layer symmetry under suitable strain. Otherwise, without strain or the in situ formed Bi layer structure, the initial structure of bulk Bi cannot be thoroughly recovered, which is in well agreement with the <i>in/</i>ex situ XRD and in situ TEM results. This can also explain the reason why both bulk and nanostructured Bi became “porous network” after repeated sodiation/desodiation reported in various literature.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406513-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0020_R_d58803839e1192" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#anie202406513-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0029_R_d58803839e1192" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10a - Adv. Mater.">10a</a>, <a href="#anie202406513-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406513-bib-0043_R_d58803839e1192" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12h - Adv. Mater.">12h</a></sup></span> Therefore, the unique particle-in-bulk BiNC structure can enable the direct transformation between Bi and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi, which significantly improves the reversibility of the layered Bi structure through the “domino-like” phase-recovering during alloying-dealloying process. It is worth to mention that, the stresses induced by the sodiation/desodiation can be partially transferred to the surrounding carbon layer, which gradually disintegrates the bulk carbon, but meanwhile prevent the Bi nanospheres from sever volume expansion. The “domino-like” phase recovering together with the stress-transfer effect could underlie the structural origin for the unprecedented cycling performance of BiNC.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202406513-sec-0009">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202406513-sec-0009-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>To summarize, we have demonstrated a novel bulk BiNC anode via a facile annealing process for efficient and durable sodium/potassium storage. We simulate the volume-expansion-resistant mechanism of bismuth during the alloying reaction with varying degrees of stiffness (corresponding to various out-of-plane stresses) and confirm that, certain compressive stress raised by the bulk carbon would enable a “domino-like” Bi crystal recovering, thus keeping the notable stability of the structure. Through comprehensive in situ TEM and in situ XRD characterizations, we have visualized for the first time the direct transformation between Bi and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi. The BiNC composite manifests a very high tap density (1.97 g cm<sup>−3</sup>), and outstanding Na/K storage performance, including an ultra-high volumetric capacity, a super-long cycle-life of 15000 cycles, and an ultrahigh rate capability up to 100 A g<sup>−1</sup>. In addition, the full cell of BiNC delivered a remarkable energy density of 226.5 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and a high-power density of 9.44 kW kg<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating its potential in practical large-scale applications. This work clarifies a long-standing puzzle for the continuous and irreversible structural evolution of Bi anode, and provides a train of thought for the rational structural design for other alloying-type anodes.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
